- `sapi_to_wav(text) -> bytes | None`: under `_sapi_lock`, creates a temp `.wav` path (`tempfile.mktemp`), opens a `SAPI.SpFileStream` COM object at 22kHz 16-bit mono (`Format.Type = 22`), redirects the voice's `AudioOutputStream` to that file stream, calls `_speaker.Speak(text, 0)` **synchronously** (flag `0`) so the call blocks until rendering completes, restores the original output stream, closes the file stream, reads the resulting bytes from disk, deletes the temp file in a `finally` block, and returns `None` if the resulting file is implausibly small (`len(data) <= 64` bytes, treated as a failed/empty render) or the bytes otherwise.
- `local_speak(text)`: fires `_speaker.Speak(text, 1)` ‚Äî flag `1` is `SVSFlagsAsync`, so this is a non-blocking, fire-and-forget announcement on the server machine's own audio output, wrapped in a bare `try/except Exception: pass` so a local speaker failure never affects client-facing behavior.

### 5.4 Piper Path (cross-platform, external CLI subprocess)

`_piper_cli = shutil.which("piper")` is resolved once at import time ‚Äî Piper is invoked as an **external command-line executable**, not a Python library binding. `piper_to_wav(text)`:
1. Returns `None` immediately if `PIPER_MODEL` is unset, the model file doesn't exist, or the `piper` executable isn't found on `PATH`.
2. Creates a temp output `.wav` path via `tempfile.NamedTemporaryFile(delete=False)`.
3. Runs `subprocess.run([piper_cli, "--model", PIPER_MODEL, "--output_file", out_path], input=text.encode("utf-8"), stdout=DEVNULL, stderr=DEVNULL, timeout=30)` ‚Äî text is piped to the Piper CLI's stdin as raw UTF-8 bytes, and Piper writes a WAV file to `out_path`. A 30-second timeout guards against a hung subprocess.
4. Reads the resulting file bytes, deletes the temp file, and returns the bytes (or `None` if empty).
5. Any exception (including `subprocess.TimeoutExpired`) is caught broadly and logged, returning `None`.

### 5.5 Unified Synthesis Entry Point

`synth_wav(text) -> tuple[bytes | None, str]` is the single function every reply passes through (called only from `feature_server._send_with_audio`). Its logic: if `TTS_ENGINE` is `"auto"` or `"piper"`, try Piper first; if Piper produced audio, return `(wav, "piper")`. If Piper was explicitly requested (`TTS_ENGINE == "piper"`) but failed, short-circuit to `(None, "piper_failed")` **without** falling back to SAPI (explicit-engine selection is treated as a hard preference, not just an ordering hint). Otherwise (either `"auto"` mode where Piper failed/was unavailable, or `TTS_ENGINE == "sapi"`), fall through to `sapi_to_wav`, returning `(wav, "sapi")` on success or `(None, "none")` on total failure. So `"auto"` mode is Piper-preferred-with-SAPI-fallback; explicit `"piper"` or `"sapi"` pins to one engine with no cross-fallback (except that requesting `"sapi"` explicitly never tries Piper at all, since the `if` only triggers Piper attempts for `"auto"`/`"piper"`).

`init_tts()` is called once at startup: selects the SAPI voice (if SAPI available), sets the SAPI rate, and prints a diagnostic report of engine readiness for both engines, including an explicit warning if **neither** engine is available (`"WARNING: no PC voice available ‚Äî client will use espeak."`) ‚Äî meaning total TTS failure on the server is a known, tolerated degraded mode: the reply text still reaches the client in the `tts` field, and the OrangePi client is expected to fall back to its own on-device `espeak` synthesis.

### 5.6 External Libraries Used by tts.py

Standard library: `os` (path checks, temp file cleanup), `shutil` (`which`, to locate the Piper CLI on `PATH`), `subprocess` (invoking the Piper CLI as an external process), `tempfile` (`mktemp`, `NamedTemporaryFile` for intermediate WAV files), `threading` (the SAPI render lock).

Third-party: `win32com.client` (from the `pywin32` package, listed in `requirements_server.txt` as `pywin32>=306 ; platform_system == "Windows"`) ‚Äî used purely for COM automation of the Windows Speech API (`SAPI.SpVoice`, `SAPI.SpFileStream`), giving Python access to Windows' built-in TTS voices without a pure-Python SAPI wrapper.

External non-Python dependency: the **Piper** neural-TTS engine itself, consumed as a standalone CLI binary located via `PATH` ‚Äî it is not a pip package and does not appear in `requirements_server.txt`; it must be separately installed/placed on the system and referenced by `--piper-model` pointing at a `.onnx` voice file. This is a notable gap between the documented Python dependency list and the actual runtime dependency set worth flagging in the report.

## 6. handlers/tts_handler.py ‚Äî Standalone TTS Feature Handler

### 6.1 Purpose

A minimal, 20-line concrete `FeatureHandler` that exposes text-to-speech as its own directly invokable feature (as opposed to the *implicit* TTS enrichment every feature gets via `_send_with_audio`). Per its docstring: the hub sends `{"feature": "tts", "text": "..."}`; this handler's job is only to validate/pass through the text ‚Äî the actual audio synthesis happens generically afterward in `feature_server._send_with_audio`, not inside this handler.

### 6.2 Implementation

```
class TTSHandler(FeatureHandler):
    name = "tts"
    def handle(self, msg: dict) -> dict:
        text = msg.get("text", "").strip()
        if not text:
            return {"tts": "", "ok": True, "quit": False}
        return {"tts": text, "ok": True, "quit": False}
```

`handle` reads `msg["text"]`, defaulting to `""` and stripping whitespace. If empty after stripping, it returns `{"tts": "", "ok": True, "quit": False}` ‚Äî a "successful no-op" (note: an empty `tts` string means `_send_with_audio` will skip synthesis entirely, since its check is `if text:`). If text is present, it echoes it straight back in the `tts` field with `ok: True, quit: False`. This handler holds **no session state** and its `quit` is always `False` ‚Äî every TTS request is stateless and the "session" (handler instance, per ¬ß3.1/¬ß4.4) simply persists uselessly in the connection's `handlers` dict for the life of the connection since nothing ever ends it (a minor architectural quirk: the TTS feature session, once opened by any message with `feature: "tts"`, never naturally closes since `quit` is hardcoded `False`; it must be evicted only by connection teardown).

### 6.3 Full TTS Round Trip

1. Hub-side: `ctx.link.send("tts", {"text": "some phrase"})` ‚Üí `ServerLink.send` sets `payload["feature"] = "tts"`, frames, sends, blocks for the reply.
2. Server accepts the framed message via `recv_msg`, sees `feature == "tts"`, looks up `TTSHandler` in `FEATURE_HANDLERS`, instantiates or reuses the cached instance for this connection, calls `.handle(msg)`.
3. `TTSHandler.handle` returns `{"tts": text, "ok": True, "quit": False}`.
4. `_send_with_audio` sees non-empty `resp["tts"]`, calls `tts.local_speak(text)` (server monitor speaker) and `tts.synth_wav(text)` (Piper-then-SAPI), attaches `resp["audio"] = base64(wav)` if synthesis succeeded, logs a TTS metric line, and calls `send_msg(conn, resp)`.
5. Hub-side: `ServerLink.send` reads back the framed JSON reply; per the docstring in `tts_handler.py`, "Hub reads `resp["audio"]` and plays it with `fb.play_raw()`."

## 7. JSON Message Schema (Request/Response Contract)

### 7.1 Request Schema (Hub ‚Üí Server)

Every request is a JSON object with:
- `feature` (string, **required** by convention though defaulted to `"money"` server-side if absent) ‚Äî the dispatch key. Injected automatically by `ServerLink.send(feature, msg)` on the client side, overwriting any `feature` key the caller may have set in `msg` (`payload["feature"] = feature`).
- Feature-specific keys, entirely handler-defined and not validated by any shared schema layer. Observed examples from the README and `tts_handler.py`: `type` (e.g., `"voice"`), `text` (spoken command text, or text to synthesize for `tts`), `frame` (raw JPEG bytes for image-based features ‚Äî automatically base64-encoded by `ServerLink.send` if passed as `bytes`/`bytearray`, decoded server-side by the individual handler, not by `protocol.py`).
- No envelope fields like a message ID, timestamp, or protocol version are part of the shared schema ‚Äî correlation between request and reply is implicit and purely positional/sequential per socket (the client blocks on `send()` until exactly one reply arrives, so there is no need for request IDs; the protocol is strictly synchronous request-reply per hub connection).

### 7.2 Response Schema (Server ‚Üí Hub)

Constructed by a handler's `handle()` and then augmented by `_send_with_audio` before transmission:
- `tts` (string, **required** by the base contract) ‚Äî text to be spoken; empty string means "say nothing."
- `quit` (bool, **required** by the base contract) ‚Äî whether this ends the feature's per-connection session.
- `ok` (bool, **optional**, used by `TTSHandler` but not mandated by `FeatureHandler`) ‚Äî an ad hoc success flag; not every handler necessarily sets it (no shared "envelope" enforcement exists in `base.py`, so each handler chooses its own extra fields).
- `audio` (string, **optional**, base64-encoded WAV bytes) ‚Äî added only by `_send_with_audio`, never by the handler itself, and only if `tts` was non-empty **and** synthesis succeeded.
- Error-path shape: when a handler raises, `feature_server.py` synthesizes `{"tts": f"{feature} error: {he}", "quit": False}` itself ‚Äî there is no distinct `"error": true`/`"status": "error"` field; errors are communicated purely as spoken/text content in the same `tts` field a success response would use, with no schema-level distinction between success and failure. This is a notable design characteristic worth calling out explicitly: the wire protocol has **no standardized error envelope** at all.
- Unknown-feature shape: `{"tts": f"Unknown feature {feature}.", "quit": False}` ‚Äî same shape, same lack of an explicit error flag.

## 8. Concrete Scenarios

1. **Successful money-feature round trip**: Hub sends `{"feature": "money", "type": "voice", "text": "scan", "frame": "<base64 jpeg>"}`. Server dispatches to (existing or newly created) `MoneyHandler` instance for this connection, which runs YOLO detection and returns e.g. `{"tts": "Twenty dollars.", "quit": False}`. `_send_with_audio` synthesizes a WAV, attaches `audio`, sends framed reply. Hub speaks the returned audio.

2. **Successful TTS-feature round trip**: as detailed in ¬ß6.3 ‚Äî `{"feature": "tts", "text": "Battery low."}` in, `{"tts": "Battery low.", "ok": true, "quit": false, "audio": "<base64 wav>"}` out.

3. **Unknown feature name**: Hub sends `{"feature": "weather", ...}`. `FEATURE_HANDLERS.get("weather")` returns `None`; server replies `{"tts": "Unknown feature weather.", "quit": False}` (with synthesized audio) and `continue`s the loop ‚Äî connection stays fully alive, no exception, no disconnect, and the local `handlers` dict for this connection is untouched.

4. **Malformed JSON / truncated frame**: If the length-prefix arrives but the JSON body is incomplete, cut off, or not valid UTF-8/JSON, `recv_msg` catches `JSONDecodeError`/`UnicodeDecodeError`, logs `"[PROTO] recv_msg: malformed payload (...) ‚Äî dropping message"`, and returns `None`. In `handle_client`, `msg is None` triggers an immediate `break` ‚Äî the entire connection is torn down (not just that one message dropped), landing in the `finally` block (`GloveState.disconnect`, `conn.close()`). Note the log message's phrasing ("dropping message") is slightly misleading relative to actual behavior, since the practical effect one layer up is termination of the whole connection, not a resumable per-message skip ‚Äî worth flagging as a documentation/behavior mismatch.

5. **Client disconnects mid-request**: If the hub closes its socket after sending a length prefix but before completing the body (or between messages), `recv_exact` observes `sock.recv()` returning `b""` and returns `None` at whichever read stage it was in; `recv_msg` returns `None`; `handle_client` breaks the loop and cleans up identically to case 4.

6. **TTS engine failure / unsupported text**: If both Piper and SAPI fail (e.g., no Piper binary, no `pywin32`, empty voice list, or a corrupt model file), `synth_wav` returns `(None, "none")`; `_send_with_audio` skips setting `resp["audio"]`, logs `"[TTS] no PC audio (none); client will use espeak."`, and still sends the reply's `tts` text field ‚Äî the round trip completes successfully from the protocol's point of view; only the optional audio enrichment is degraded. There is no language/locale negotiation anywhere in this code ‚Äî whatever text a handler produces is handed to whichever engine is configured, with no validation of script/language compatibility; a non-Latin-script or non-English string would simply be mis-pronounced or fail per the underlying engine's own error handling (e.g., Piper might error and be caught by its broad `except Exception`, returning `None`; SAPI would attempt best-effort pronunciation).

7. **Concurrent clients/connections**: Each accepted socket runs in its own daemon thread with its own local `handlers` dict, so feature sessions are fully isolated per connection. Shared mutable state across threads is limited to: `GloveState` (guarded by `GloveState._lock`), the TTS metrics file (guarded by `_tts_metrics_lock`), and the SAPI COM object (guarded by `tts._sapi_lock`, since a single COM `SpVoice` instance is shared process-wide and is documented as not safely reentrant). Piper synthesis, by contrast, spawns an independent subprocess per call and is not serialized by any lock ‚Äî concurrent Piper renders from multiple connections can run in true parallel (bounded only by CPU/OS process limits).

8. **Handler exception / server crash**: An exception raised inside `handler.handle(msg)` is caught locally in `handle_client`'s per-message try block; it does **not** propagate to crash the connection thread or the server process. The traceback is printed server-side for diagnostics, `GloveState.error()` increments an error counter for that feature, and the client receives a spoken/text error message (`"{feature} error: {he}"`) with `quit: False`, meaning the client can immediately retry the same feature on the same still-open connection. Only exceptions escaping the entire message loop (e.g., a raw socket-level error not related to a specific handler call) hit the outer `try/except Exception as e`, which logs and falls through to the same cleanup `finally` block ‚Äî even in that case, only the single affected connection's thread is affected; other connections' threads and the server process itself are unaffected, since there is no shared state whose corruption could propagate, and thread-per-connection isolation means one thread's unhandled exception cannot terminate the process's main thread or the accept loop.

## 9. Shared Registry Pattern for money.py, ocr.py, lidar.py

Although `handlers/money.py`, `handlers/ocr.py`, and `handlers/lidar.py` are documented in a separate report section, this interface layer is precisely what they all plug into, with zero special-casing:
- Each defines a class inheriting `handlers.base.FeatureHandler`, overriding `name` and `handle(self, msg) -> dict`.
- `feature_server.py` imports the module (`from handlers import money, ocr`; `from handlers.lidar import LidarHandler`) and inserts the class object into `FEATURE_HANDLERS` under its string key (`"money": money.MoneyHandler`, `"ocr": ocr.OCRHandler`, `"lidar": LidarHandler`, alongside `"tts": TTSHandler`).
- Dispatch, instantiation-per-connection-per-feature, exception isolation, and TTS/audio enrichment in `_send_with_audio` are all completely generic across these four handlers ‚Äî `handle_client` has no `if feature == "money"` branching anywhere; the only feature-specific code paths are inside each handler's own `handle()` method and in `main()`'s optional CLI-driven `.configure()`/preload calls for `money`/`ocr` (which are module-level setup functions, not part of the `FeatureHandler` interface itself, and are unique to those two modules ‚Äî `lidar` and `tts` have no equivalent configure/preload step visible in `feature_server.py`).
- This means the entire "core" documented in this section (protocol framing, socket accept loop, thread-per-connection model, handler registry dict, TTS enrichment, error containment, `GloveState` tracking) is a single shared mechanism ‚Äî every feature-specific report section should treat `feature_server.py`, `protocol.py`, and `handlers/base.py` as already fully specifying "how my handler gets called and how my reply gets to the client," with nothing left to redefine per feature.

## 10. The Authoritative Client-Server Wire Contract

This is the complete, end-to-end path a single request/response pair takes, naming every real function/class on both sides:

1. **Client call site**: OrangePi feature code (e.g., `features/money_recognition.py`, or generically anything holding a `FeatureContext` from `features/base.py`) calls `ctx.link.send(feature_name, msg_dict)`, where `ctx.link` is a `net.client.ServerLink` instance.
2. **ServerLink.send(feature, msg)** (`OrangePI/net/client.py`): acquires `self._lock` (serializing all sends from this hub ‚Äî one in-flight request at a time per hub); lazily calls `self.connect()` (a blocking `socket.create_connection((host, port), timeout=20)` followed by `settimeout(self.timeout)`, default 120s) if not already connected; copies `msg` into `payload`, sets `payload["feature"] = feature` (this **always overwrites** any `feature` key already in `msg`); if `payload.get("frame")` is `bytes`/`bytearray`, base64-encodes it in place so it survives JSON serialization.
3. **Client-side framing/send**: `data = json.dumps(payload).encode("utf-8")`; `self.sock.sendall(struct.pack(">I", len(data)) + data)` ‚Äî identical framing to `protocol.send_msg` on the server, just inlined rather than imported (the two modules are independent implementations of the same format, not a shared library).
4. **TCP transport**: bytes cross the network as a plain, unencrypted, unauthenticated TCP stream (no TLS, no auth handshake, no protocol version negotiation anywhere in either implementation).
5. **Server-side accept/read**: the connection was previously accepted in `feature_server.main()`'s accept loop and handed to a dedicated thread running `handle_client(conn, addr)`, which is blocked in `msg = recv_msg(conn)` (`protocol.py`) ‚Äî reading the 4-byte length via `recv_exact`, then the JSON body, then `json.loads`.
6. **Dispatch**: `handle_client` reads `msg.get("feature", "money")`, looks it up in the module-level `FEATURE_HANDLERS` dict, obtains or creates the connection-local `FeatureHandler` subclass instance, and calls `handler.handle(msg)` inside a try/except.
7. **Reply construction**: the handler returns a dict with at least `tts`/`quit`; `_send_with_audio` enriches it with a base64 `audio` field via `tts.synth_wav`.
8. **Server-side framing/send**: `protocol.send_msg(conn, resp)` ‚Äî `json.dumps` ‚Üí `struct.pack(">I", len)` prefix ‚Üí `sock.sendall`.
9. **Client-side read-back**: still inside the same `ServerLink.send()` call (which has been blocked this whole time holding `self._lock`), the client reads the 4-byte length prefix and body via its own private `_recv_exact`, then `json.loads`s the body. Any read failure (`None` from `_recv_exact`) or JSON decode failure calls `self._reset()` (closes and nulls `self.sock`, forcing a fresh `connect()` on the next `send()`) and returns `None` to the caller in place of a reply dict.
10. **Return to feature code**: `ServerLink.send` returns the decoded reply dict (or `None` on any failure at any stage), and the calling feature code is responsible for handling a `None` reply (e.g., treating it as "server unreachable, use local fallback such as espeak").

This full loop is strictly synchronous and blocking on both ends per message: the hub thread blocks inside `send()` until exactly one reply is read (bounded by the 120-second socket timeout), and the server thread blocks on `recv_msg` until the next message arrives, with no pipelining, multiplexing, or out-of-order replies possible on a single connection ‚Äî one hub, one socket, one request in flight at a time, arbitrarily many hubs concurrently via one server thread each.

## 11. Consolidated Third-Party/External Library Reference

- **`ultralytics`** (`>=8.3.0`, per `requirements_server.txt`) ‚Äî YOLO object detection, used by the money feature (declared at the shared requirements level).
- **`easyocr`** (`>=1.7.0`) ‚Äî OCR engine (torch-based), used by the OCR feature. An earlier pass of this report flagged a discrepancy where the deployed `ocr.py` code actually used `paddleocr` despite this file declaring `easyocr` ‚Äî that discrepancy has since been resolved: live testing proved PaddleOCR's GPU wheel cannot coexist with PyTorch/YOLO in this process on Windows (DLL conflict, see Section 7's OCR handler subsection for the full diagnosis), so `ocr.py` was switched to EasyOCR to match what this requirements file always specified, and `paddleocr`/`paddlepaddle-gpu`/`paddlex` plus their orphaned `nvidia-*-cu12` dependencies were uninstalled from the server environment.
- **`opencv-python`** (`>=4.10.0`) ‚Äî image processing, `cv2`, needed for both YOLO and EasyOCR pipelines.
- **`numpy`** (`>=1.24,<3.0`) ‚Äî numeric array backend underpinning OpenCV/YOLO/EasyOCR.
- **`pywin32`** (`>=306`, Windows-only via environment marker) ‚Äî `win32com.client` COM automation used exclusively by `tts.py` for SAPI speech synthesis.
- **PyTorch** (`torch`/`torchvision`/`torchaudio`, CUDA 12.1 build) ‚Äî installed as a separate manual step (not in `requirements_server.txt` proper) to back YOLO and EasyOCR GPU inference.
- **Piper** ‚Äî external CLI binary (not a pip package, absent from `requirements_server.txt`), located via `shutil.which("piper")`, invoked via `subprocess.run`; a real infrastructure dependency not captured in the documented requirements file.
- Everything in `feature_server.py`, `protocol.py`, and `handlers/base.py`/`__init__.py` themselves is pure Python standard library (`socket`, `threading`, `struct`, `json`, `argparse`, `signal`, `base64`, `time`, `os`, `traceback`).

---

## DIAGRAM GENERATION PROMPT ‚Äî Server Core, Protocol & Client-Server Interface

Generate a detailed sequence/architecture diagram (Mermaid sequence diagram plus an adjoining component box) showing the OrangePi-to-laptop request/response flow and handler registry. On the left, show the OrangePi process with a `Feature` object (from `features/base.py`, e.g. `MoneyRecognitionFeature`) holding a `FeatureContext` whose `ctx.link` is a `ServerLink` instance (`net/client.py`); show `ServerLink.send(feature, msg)` acquiring an internal lock, calling `connect()` if needed, base64-encoding a `frame` field, then framing the JSON with a 4-byte big-endian length prefix and calling `sock.sendall`. Draw the TCP wire crossing to the right side, a laptop process labeled `feature_server.py`, showing its `main()` startup creating a listening `socket`, an accept loop spawning one daemon `threading.Thread` per connection running `handle_client(conn, addr)`. Inside `handle_client`, show the call to `protocol.recv_msg(conn)` (built from `recv_exact` reading the length prefix then the body and `json.loads`), then a lookup into a `FEATURE_HANDLERS` dict (keys: `"money"`, `"ocr"`, `"tts"`, `"lidar"`, mapping to `MoneyHandler`, `OCRHandler`, `TTSHandler`, `LidarHandler`, all subclassing `handlers.base.FeatureHandler`), then instantiation/reuse of a per-connection-per-feature handler instance and a call to `handler.handle(msg)` returning a dict with `tts`/`quit`/`ok` keys. Show the reply passing through `_send_with_audio()`, which calls `tts.synth_wav(text)` (trying `piper_to_wav` via an external Piper CLI subprocess first, falling back to `sapi_to_wav` via `win32com.client`/SAPI), attaches a base64 `audio` field, and calls `protocol.send_msg(conn, resp)` to write the framed JSON reply back over the same TCP socket. Close the sequence with `ServerLink.send` on the OrangePi side reading the 4-byte length prefix and body back and returning the parsed reply dict to the calling feature. Include a small side annotation box showing the two error paths that do NOT crash the connection: unknown `feature` key returns `{"tts": "Unknown feature X.", "quit": false}`, and a handler exception is caught and turned into `{"tts": "<feature> error: <e>", "quit": false}` while the connection loop continues.

================================================================================
SECTION 7 ‚Äî SERVER-SIDE FEATURE HANDLERS: LIDAR, OCR, MONEY RECOGNITION
================================================================================

# Server-Side Feature Handlers: LiDAR, OCR, and Money Recognition

This section documents three concrete `FeatureHandler` implementations that live in `server_app/handlers/`: `lidar.py`, `ocr.py`, and `money.py`. All three are invoked by the core dispatcher (`feature_server.py`, documented elsewhere) after it decodes a length-prefixed TCP frame into a JSON message dict and reads the `feature` key to select a handler instance. Each handler receives the full message dict via its `handle(msg)` method and returns a response dict that the dispatcher serializes back over the same connection (attaching synthesized audio where relevant) using `protocol.send_msg`.

---

## 1. LiDAR Handler (`handlers/lidar.py`)

### 1.1 Purpose & Role

`LidarHandler` is not an obstacle-detection engine itself ‚Äî the SLAM/obstacle-avoidance computation happens on the OrangePi side (in the `lidar_nav.py` / `slam_engine.py` counterpart module documented elsewhere). This server-side handler is a **sink and visualization service**: it receives pre-rendered map images, pose updates, and session summary reports from the robot, persists them to disk, and exposes a live HTTP dashboard (on port 8080+) so a human can watch mapping/navigation/obstacle-avoidance sessions in a browser in near-real time. It also acts as a generic state bulletin board (`feature_state` action) that other features (OCR, money) can push status into, displayed at `/state`.

### 1.2 Pipeline Walkthrough

Entry point: `LidarHandler.handle(msg: dict) -> dict`, `name = "lidar"`. On every call it first calls `_start_http()`, which lazily starts a daemon thread running a `BaseHTTPRequestHandler`-based server on the first free port in `8080..8089` (guarded by `_http_started`/`_http_lock` so it only starts once per process). It then reads `msg["action"]` (default `"map_save"`) and dispatches to one of five private methods:

1. **`_map_save(msg, announce)`** ‚Äî used for both `map_save` (announce=True, permanent room capture) and `map_update` (announce=False, silent live refresh). Reads `room_name` (default `"room"`) and `frame` (base64 PNG string) from `msg`. If `frame` is empty, returns a no-op response immediately. Otherwise base64-decodes it (`base64.b64decode`); a decode exception is caught and returned as a spoken error (`"Bad map data: {e}"`) rather than propagating. On success it writes the raw PNG bytes straight to `MAPS_DIR/<room_name>.png` via `Path.write_bytes` ‚Äî note there is no re-encoding, resizing, or annotation of the map image itself; the OrangePi side has already rendered the PNG. `map_save` prints a confirmation with the viewer URL and returns `{"tts": "Map saved for <room>.", "quit": False}`; `map_update` is silent (`tts: ""`) so the TTS layer doesn't narrate every frame refresh.
2. **`_pose_update(msg)`** ‚Äî reads `room_name`, `x`, `y` (meters, cast to `float`). If the named room's PNG already exists on disk, converts world coordinates to pixel coordinates via `_world_to_px` (uses `_MAP_RES = 0.05` m/px and `_MAP_ORIG = 15.0` m, which must match constants in the OrangePi's `slam_engine.py`) and stores `(col, row)` in the in-memory dict `_live[room]` under a `threading.Lock`. Always returns a silent no-op response ‚Äî this is a pure state update, not something spoken.
3. **`_report(msg)`** ‚Äî reads `mode` (e.g. `"obstacles"`, `"mapping"`, `"navigate"`), `session_id`, and `data` (an arbitrary nested dict produced by the OrangePi session). Writes `{"mode","session_id","data"}` as pretty-printed JSON (`json.dumps(..., indent=2)`) to `REPORTS_DIR/<session_id>_<mode>.json`. Returns `{"tts": "<mode> report saved.", "quit": False}`.
4. **`_feature_state(msg)`** ‚Äî reads `feature_name` as a key and stores all other message fields (excluding `feature`/`action`) into the shared `_feature_state` dict for display on `/state`. Silent response.
5. Any other/unknown action falls through to a silent `{"tts": "", "quit": False}`.

The **HTTP viewer** (inner class `_H(BaseHTTPRequestHandler)`) is a read path that never touches the TCP protocol; it serves: `/` or `/maps` (index of saved rooms), `/live?room=X` (auto-refresh `<img>` page, JS polls `/map/<room>.png` every 1.5 s), `/map/<room>.png` (reads the stored PNG, and if a live pose exists for that room, calls `_overlay()` to draw a red/white dot via OpenCV before serving; validates the room name against `re.fullmatch(r'[\w\-]+', ...)` to prevent path traversal; serves a synthesized "Waiting for data‚Ä¶" placeholder PNG if the file doesn't exist yet), `/reports` (list of saved JSON reports by file size), `/report/<filename>` (renders one report as HTML via `_render_report`, dispatching on `mode` to `_render_obstacles`/`_render_mapping`/`_render_navigate`, each producing an HTML table of statistics, motor-fire counts, keyframe logs, or pose logs), `/metrics` (reads `ocr_metrics.jsonl`, `money_metrics.jsonl`, `tts_metrics.jsonl` ‚Äî the sibling handlers' metrics files ‚Äî and renders the last 50 lines of each as HTML tables, demonstrating that the LiDAR handler's HTTP server doubles as the shared metrics dashboard for all three features), and `/state` (renders `_feature_state`).

### 1.3 Third-Party / Standard Libraries

- `base64` ‚Äî decode incoming PNG map frames; decode/encode the fallback placeholder image.
- `json` ‚Äî serialize/deserialize report payloads (`json.dumps`/`json.loads`).
- `math` ‚Äî imported but not directly used in the reviewed code path (SLAM math lives on the OrangePi side); reserved/vestigial here.
- `re` ‚Äî validates `room` path segments in `_serve_png` (`re.fullmatch(r'[\w\-]+', ...)`) to reject unsafe filenames.
- `socket` ‚Äî resolves the LAN IP (`socket.gethostbyname(socket.gethostname())`) purely for the printed viewer URL.
- `threading` ‚Äî `Lock` objects guard `_live` (pose cache) and `_feature_state`; a daemon `Thread` runs the HTTP server so it doesn't block the TCP feature-server loop.
- `http.server` (`BaseHTTPRequestHandler`, `HTTPServer`) ‚Äî the entire live-viewer web server.
- `pathlib.Path` ‚Äî all filesystem paths (`MAPS_DIR`, `REPORTS_DIR`), directory creation (`mkdir(exist_ok=True)`), globbing (`glob("*.png")`, `glob("*.json")`), and byte-level read/write (`read_bytes`, `write_bytes`, `write_text`).
- `urllib.parse` (`parse_qs`, `urlparse`) ‚Äî parses query strings (e.g. `?room=`) from HTTP GET requests.
- `cv2` (OpenCV, imported lazily inside `_waiting_png()` and `_overlay()`) ‚Äî draws the "Waiting for data..." placeholder text (`cv2.putText`), decodes/re-encodes PNG bytes (`cv2.imdecode`, `cv2.imencode`), converts grayscale to BGR (`cv2.cvtColor`), and draws the position dot (`cv2.circle`, filled red + white outline).
- `numpy` (lazy import alongside cv2) ‚Äî wraps raw PNG bytes into a byte buffer (`np.frombuffer`) for OpenCV decoding, and allocates the placeholder image array (`np.full`).
- `handlers.base.FeatureHandler` ‚Äî the shared contract base class.

### 1.4 Key Classes/Functions, Parameters, Returns, Side Effects

- `_start_http() -> None` ‚Äî side effect: spawns daemon thread `lidar-http`; creates `MAPS_DIR`/`REPORTS_DIR` if missing; binds first free TCP port 8080‚Äì8089.
- `LidarHandler.handle(msg: dict) -> dict` ‚Äî pure dispatch, no direct side effects besides calling `_start_http()`.
- `LidarHandler._map_save(msg: dict, announce: bool) -> dict` ‚Äî **disk write**: `MAPS_DIR/<room_name>.png`, overwritten in place (no timestamp in filename ‚Äî one file per room, always latest).
- `LidarHandler._pose_update(msg: dict) -> dict` ‚Äî in-memory only, no disk write.
- `LidarHandler._report(msg: dict) -> dict` ‚Äî **disk write**: `REPORTS_DIR/<session_id>_<mode>.json`, pretty JSON, overwritten if same session_id+mode recurs.
- `LidarHandler._feature_state(msg: dict) -> dict` ‚Äî in-memory only.
- `_world_to_px(x_m, y_m) -> (int, int)` ‚Äî pure coordinate transform.
- `_overlay(base_png: bytes, pos) -> bytes` ‚Äî pure transform, returns original bytes unchanged if `pos is None` or on any OpenCV exception (fail-open behavior).

### 1.5 Data Structures

Request schema (fields read from `msg`, all optional with defaults):
- `feature` = `"lidar"` (used by the outer dispatcher, not read inside this file)
- `action` ‚àà `{"map_save","map_update","pose_update","report","feature_state"}` (default `"map_save"`)
- For `map_save`/`map_update`: `room_name` (str), `frame` (base64-encoded PNG string)
- For `pose_update`: `room_name` (str), `x` (float meters), `y` (float meters)
- For `report`: `mode` (str: `"obstacles"|"mapping"|"navigate"|other`), `session_id` (str), `data` (arbitrary nested dict specific to the mode)
- For `feature_state`: `feature_name` (str, used as dict key), `state` (str, logged), plus arbitrary extra keys that get stored verbatim

Response schema: always `{"tts": str, "quit": bool}` ‚Äî never any structured payload (no maps, coordinates, or obstacle lists are returned to the caller; this handler is fire-and-forget/persist-only).

### 1.6 Concrete Scenarios

- **Normal map_save**: valid base64 PNG ‚Üí file written, spoken confirmation, viewer URL printed to console.
- **Empty frame**: `frame` missing/empty ‚Üí silent no-op response, no file written, no exception.
- **Malformed base64**: `base64.b64decode` raises ‚Üí caught, returned as a spoken error string, no crash, no partial file.
- **pose_update for unknown room**: room PNG doesn't exist yet ‚Üí pose silently dropped (not cached), no error.
- **report with unknown mode**: `_render_report` falls back to a raw `<pre>{json.dumps(...)}</pre>` dump rather than a formatted table ‚Äî never errors on unexpected report shapes.
- **HTTP artifact read failure**: `/report/<filename>` catches JSON-parse exceptions and renders `<pre>Error reading report: {e}</pre>` instead of a 500.
- **Repeated rapid map_update calls** (live mapping): each overwrites the same `<room>.png`; the `/live` page polls every 1.5 s so intermediate frames may be skipped ‚Äî no queuing or backpressure, last-write-wins.
- **Disk full / permission error on write**: `write_bytes`/`write_text` would raise unhandled in `_map_save`/`_report` (not wrapped in try/except at that layer) ‚Äî this would propagate up to the dispatcher's own exception handling (documented elsewhere), unlike OCR/Money which wrap their equivalent risky operations more defensively at the metric-logging layer only.

### 1.7 Contract & Counterpart

`LidarHandler(FeatureHandler)` sets `name = "lidar"` and implements `handle()`; the dispatcher's registry (in `feature_server.py`, documented elsewhere) maps incoming `msg["feature"] == "lidar"` to an instance of this class ‚Äî `handlers/__init__.py` itself is empty, so the registry wiring is centralized in the core server file rather than via decorators here. The OrangePi-side counterpart is `lidar_nav.py` (with `slam_engine.py` producing the actual SLAM map/pose), which sends `map_save`/`map_update`/`pose_update`/`report`/`feature_state` action messages with the `feature: "lidar"` tag; the shared `_MAP_RES`/`_MAP_ORIG` constants are a hard-coded contract between the two sides that must stay in sync.

---

## 2. OCR Handler (`handlers/ocr.py`)

### 2.1 Purpose & Role

Implements the "Book Reader" capability: given a JPEG frame of a printed page, it detects and recognizes text, identifies a probable page number, and returns both raw text and pre-chunked, TTS-ready audio so the OrangePi glove can read a page aloud incrementally. This is the read-aloud/accessibility feature of the smart-glove system.

### 2.2 Pipeline Walkthrough

Entry point: `OCRHandler.handle(msg: dict) -> dict`, `name = "ocr"`. Reads `msg["type"]` (default `"scan"`): `"hello"` returns an empty ack; `"scan"` routes to `_handle_scan`; anything else returns `"Unknown OCR command."`.

`_handle_scan(msg)`:
1. Reads `msg["frame"]` (base64 JPEG). Missing ‚Üí logs error and returns `"No image received."` immediately (no file write, no exception).
2. `_decode_jpeg(b64)`: `base64.b64decode` ‚Üí `np.frombuffer` ‚Üí `cv2.imdecode(..., cv2.IMREAD_COLOR)`. Any exception anywhere is swallowed and `None` returned. `None` result ‚Üí logs `"decode_failed"` and returns `"Could not decode image."`.
3. **Debug artifact #1 (raw)**: writes the decoded, un-preprocessed image to `ocr_debug/scan_<YYYYMMDD_HHMMSS>_raw.jpg` via `cv2.imwrite`, unconditionally, before any further processing ‚Äî this is the "raw" debug jpg mentioned in the task.
4. `_preprocess(img)`: `_auto_rotate` rotates portrait images (height > width) 90¬∞ counter-clockwise to landscape so text lines run horizontally; then if width < 1000 px, upscales with `cv2.resize(..., interpolation=cv2.INTER_CUBIC)`; then applies an **unsharp-mask sharpening** step ‚Äî `cv2.GaussianBlur(img, (0,0), sigma=3)` followed by `cv2.addWeighted(img, 1.5, blur, -0.5, 0)` ‚Äî to sharpen text edges before OCR.
5. **Debug artifact #2 (proc)**: writes the preprocessed image to `ocr_debug/scan_<timestamp>_proc.jpg` (same timestamp as the raw file, computed once at the top of `_handle_scan`). Immediately after, calls `_rotate_debug()`, which lists all `.jpg` files in `ocr_debug/` sorted by mtime and deletes every file beyond the newest `MAX_DEBUG_FILES = 20`, keeping the debug directory bounded; deletion errors (`OSError`) are silently ignored per-file.
6. `_run_ocr(processed)`: dispatches to whichever engine `OCR_ENGINE` selects (set via `feature_server.py --ocr-engine {easyocr,paddleocr}`, default `easyocr`), with automatic fallback ‚Äî see the dual-engine subsection below. Either path returns the same `(bbox, text, conf)` tuple shape this function needs, so the rest of the handler (sorting, threshold filtering, page detection, chunking) is engine-agnostic.

**Engine choice ‚Äî dual-engine design with automatic fallback (see also Section 9.3 metrics notes).** This handler supports two OCR engines, selected at server startup:

  - `"easyocr"` (default) ‚Äî a singleton `easyocr.Reader(["en"], gpu=use_gpu, verbose=False)` via `_get_easyocr()` (thread-safe via `_easyocr_lock`), GPU/CPU auto-detected from `torch.cuda.is_available()`. Torch-based, so it shares PyTorch's CUDA runtime with `handlers/money.py`'s YOLO ‚Äî no conflict. `readtext(img)` already returns the exact `(bbox, text, conf)` shape needed, no translation required. Confirmed working on GPU on the deployed server.
  - `"paddleocr"` ‚Äî deliberately NOT run in-process. It's spawned as an isolated subprocess, `paddle_ocr_worker.py`, communicating over a localhost-only TCP socket using the exact same 4-byte length-prefixed JSON framing as `protocol.py` (imported directly by both sides ‚Äî stdlib-only, safe to share across processes). `_ensure_paddle_worker()` spawns it (`--device gpu:0` first, falling back to `--device cpu` if that fails to come up within a 90 s startup window), and `_run_paddle_ocr(img)` JPEG-encodes the frame, base64s it, and round-trips it to the worker for each request.

**Why isolation, and why it currently doesn't help on this deployment.** The original hypothesis was that PaddlePaddle-GPU's bundled CUDA 12 runtime conflicts with PyTorch's bundled runtime once `torch` is imported in the same process (as `handlers/money.py` always does) ‚Äî process isolation was built specifically to eliminate that. Extensive direct testing during development proved this hypothesis **incomplete**: the actual failure (`OSError: [WinError 127] The specified procedure could not be found` loading `cusparse64_12.dll`) reproduces identically:
  - with **and without** `torch` imported anywhere in the process,
  - in **both** GPU (`device="gpu:0"`) and **CPU** (`device="cpu"`) modes,
  - on **both** `paddlepaddle-gpu==3.0.0rc1` (cu123) and the current stable `paddlepaddle-gpu==3.3.1` (cu126, exactly matched to the machine's driver via `nvidia-smi`),
  - with a confirmed-intact Microsoft Visual C++ Redistributable (`vcruntime140.dll`/`msvcp140.dll`/`vcruntime140_1.dll` all load fine),
  - regardless of whether the system-wide NVIDIA CUDA Toolkit directory is present on `PATH`.

  Most tellingly: manually replicating paddle's own documented Windows DLL-bootstrap sequence from `paddle/__init__.py` ‚Äî every DLL in `paddle/libs` (its legacy bundled MKL/BLAS libraries) plus every DLL in all nine `nvidia-*-cu12` subpackage `bin/` directories, loaded via the identical `kernel32.LoadLibraryExW(dll, None, 0x1100)` calls after registering all directories via `os.add_dll_directory()` ‚Äî succeeds **completely**, every single DLL, in isolation. Yet the real `import paddle` executing that exact same code still crashes. This means the actual defect is inside paddle's compiled core module loading on this specific machine, in a way not observable or patchable from the Python level ‚Äî a genuine, reproducible upstream/environment bug, not a version-pinning or torch-conflict issue as first suspected.

  `_ensure_paddle_worker()` therefore treats a PaddleOCR worker start failure as an expected, handled outcome rather than a fatal error: it tries GPU then CPU once, logs the failure to `feature_metrics.jsonl` (`device: "paddleocr"`, `ok: false`), sets a module-level `_paddle_available = False` flag so it does **not** retry the worker on every subsequent request (avoiding a repeated ~15 s two-attempt startup cost per OCR scan), and `_run_ocr()` falls through to `_get_easyocr()` transparently. The net effect: passing `--ocr-engine paddleocr` is always safe ‚Äî worst case, it behaves identically to `--ocr-engine easyocr` after one slightly slower first request, with no crash and no repeated cost. If the underlying paddle bug is ever fixed upstream, or if `paddlepaddle-gpu` is downgraded to the pre-paddlex 2.x line (`paddle_ocr_worker.py`'s `_get_engine()`/`_run_ocr()` both already have a fallback code path for PaddleOCR 2.x's older `use_angle_cls`/`use_gpu`/`.ocr(img, cls=True)` API, via a `TypeError`/`hasattr` check), `--ocr-engine paddleocr` will start actually using PaddleOCR with no further code changes on either side.
7. If zero raw detections ‚Üí logs `{"words":0,...,"error":"no_detections"}` and returns empty text (`{"tts": "", "text": "", "quit": False}`).
8. Detections are sorted top-to-bottom using each bbox's top-left y-coordinate (`raw.sort(key=lambda r: r[0][0][1])`), then filtered by `CONF_THRESHOLD = 0.30`. If nothing survives filtering ‚Üí logs `"all_below_threshold"` and again returns empty text.
9. On success: joins surviving lines into `full_text`, computes `word_count`, and calls `_detect_page(lines)` ‚Äî a regex-based page-number heuristic (`_PAGE_PATTERNS`: `"page N"`, `"pg. N"`, a bare-number line, `"N of M"`, or `"- N -"`) that searches the bottom quarter of detected lines first (most common page-number placement), falling back to the whole page, accepting only integers in `[1, 9999]`.
10. Logs a full metrics record (`words`, `lines`, `page`, `conf_avg`, `detections_total`, `detections_kept`, `processing_ms`, `error: None`) to `ocr_metrics.jsonl`.
11. Builds a spoken `announcement` ("Page N. W words." or "No page number. W words."), chunks the full text via `_chunk_text` (splits on sentence boundaries with `re.split(r'(?<=[.!?])\s+', ...)`, then further splits long sentences on commas/semicolons targeting ~`_CHUNK_TARGET = 10` words per chunk, capped display length `MAX_CHUNK_TTS = 20` words), and synthesizes WAV audio for the announcement and for each chunk (up to 20) via `_synth()`, which calls an optional `tts` module (`tts.synth_wav`) if importable ‚Äî TTS failures are caught and logged, returning `None` for that clip without failing the whole scan.
12. Final response includes `announcement_wav`/`chunk_wavs` only if synthesis succeeded (base64-encoded WAV bytes), keeping the payload minimal when TTS is unavailable.

### 2.3 Third-Party / Standard Libraries

- `base64` ‚Äî decode incoming JPEG frame; encode outgoing WAV clips (`announcement_wav`, `chunk_wavs`).
- `json` ‚Äî one-line-per-record metrics logging (`ocr_metrics.jsonl`).
- `os` ‚Äî path joining/dirname (`os.path.dirname(__file__)`), directory creation (`os.makedirs(..., exist_ok=True)`), directory scanning and deletion in `_rotate_debug` (`os.scandir`, `entry.stat().st_mtime`, `os.unlink`).
- `re` ‚Äî page-number regex patterns; sentence/clause splitting for TTS chunking.
- `threading` ‚Äî `Lock`s for metrics file writes and OCR-engine singleton initialization (concurrency safety across simultaneous connections).
- `time` ‚Äî timestamps for filenames (`time.strftime("%Y%m%d_%H%M%S")`) and metrics (`time.strftime("%Y-%m-%d %H:%M:%S")`), and latency measurement (`time.time()` before/after for `processing_ms`).
- `cv2` (OpenCV, `opencv-python` in requirements) ‚Äî JPEG decode/encode-adjacent ops: `imdecode`, `rotate` (90¬∞ CCW), `resize` (cubic upscaling), `GaussianBlur` + `addWeighted` (unsharp mask), `imwrite` (both debug dumps).
- `numpy` ‚Äî `frombuffer` to wrap raw decoded bytes for OpenCV; implicit array typing throughout (`np.ndarray`).
- `easyocr.Reader` ‚Äî the default OCR engine (text detection + recognition, multi-lingual, initialized here for `["en"]` only). Confirmed via direct testing to share PyTorch's CUDA runtime cleanly. Lazy-imported inside `_build_easyocr()`, not at module top level, so the package need not be present if `--ocr-engine paddleocr` is used exclusively (though the fallback logic means it should generally stay installed).
- `paddleocr.PaddleOCR` ‚Äî the optional engine, imported and used only inside the separate `paddle_ocr_worker.py` process, never inside `handlers/ocr.py`'s own process. See the "dual-engine design" note above for why it's isolated and why it currently doesn't help on this deployment.
- `subprocess`, `sys`, `socket` (aliased `_socket`) ‚Äî spawning and talking to the isolated `paddle_ocr_worker.py` process (`_spawn_worker`, `_worker_ping`, `_run_paddle_ocr`).
- `protocol.recv_msg`/`send_msg` ‚Äî reused directly (not reimplemented) for the worker IPC, the same 4-byte length-prefixed JSON framing documented in Section 6.
- `torch` (lazy import inside `_get_easyocr` and `_ensure_paddle_worker`) ‚Äî only used to probe `torch.cuda.is_available()` for GPU/CPU device selection; import failure is caught and treated as CPU-only.
- `tts` (optional local module, imported at top with `try/except`) ‚Äî `tts.synth_wav(text) -> (wav_bytes, engine_name)`; entire feature degrades gracefully to text-only responses if unavailable.
- `handlers.base.FeatureHandler` ‚Äî base contract.

### 2.4 Key Classes/Functions

- `configure(engine: str | None) -> None` ‚Äî sets the module-level `OCR_ENGINE` (`"easyocr"` or `"paddleocr"`), called once from `feature_server.py`'s `main()` based on `--ocr-engine`.
- `preload() -> None` ‚Äî call at server startup to warm the configured engine (and EasyOCR too, as the fallback, if PaddleOCR was requested but its worker can't start) before the first real request.
- `_get_easyocr() -> easyocr.Reader` ‚Äî singleton accessor, thread-safe (`_easyocr_lock`), lazy-loads on first call, prints device selection, retries once on CPU if GPU construction raises, and logs a `model_load` entry (`ok`, `processing_ms`, `device`) to the consolidated `handlers/feature_metrics.jsonl` (Section 9).
- `_ensure_paddle_worker() -> bool` ‚Äî thread-safe (`_worker_lock`), spawns `paddle_ocr_worker.py` (GPU then CPU attempt), pings it to confirm readiness within a 90 s window, and ‚Äî critically ‚Äî sets `_paddle_available = False` after one failure so it is **not** retried on every subsequent OCR request; returns whether the worker is currently usable.
- `_run_paddle_ocr(img) -> list[(bbox, text, conf)]` ‚Äî JPEG-encodes and base64s the frame, round-trips it to the worker over a fresh local TCP connection per request, and normalizes the reply into the standard tuple shape.
- `_decode_jpeg(b64: str) -> np.ndarray | None` ‚Äî decode helper, fail-safe (returns `None`).
- `_auto_rotate(img) -> np.ndarray`, `_preprocess(img) -> np.ndarray` ‚Äî image conditioning pipeline.
- `_run_ocr(img) -> list[(bbox, text, conf)]` ‚Äî the engine-dispatching entry point: tries PaddleOCR if configured and available, otherwise (or on failure) uses EasyOCR.
- `_detect_page(lines: list) -> int | None` ‚Äî page-number heuristic.
- `_chunk_text(text: str) -> list[str]` ‚Äî TTS chunking.
- `_synth(text: str) -> bytes | None` ‚Äî TTS synthesis wrapper.
- `_log_metric(rec: dict) -> None` ‚Äî appends JSON line to `ocr_metrics.jsonl`, timestamped, swallows `OSError`.
- `_rotate_debug() -> None` ‚Äî bounds `ocr_debug/` to `MAX_DEBUG_FILES = 20` newest `.jpg` files.
- **Side effects summary**: every scan writes exactly two JPEGs (`ocr_debug/scan_<ts>_raw.jpg`, `ocr_debug/scan_<ts>_proc.jpg`) regardless of OCR outcome (even on eventual "no detections"), then prunes old debug files; every scan appends one line to `ocr_metrics.jsonl` regardless of outcome (success or every category of failure) with an `error` field distinguishing the case.

### 2.5 Data Structures

Request schema: `type` (`"hello"|"scan"`), `frame` (base64 JPEG string, required for `"scan"`).

Response schema (varies by outcome, always includes `tts` and `quit`):
- Base: `{"tts": "", "quit": False}`
- Error/empty cases add `"text"`: `"No image received."`, `"Could not decode image."`, `"OCR error: <e>"`, or `""` (no detections / all below threshold).
- Success: `{"tts": "", "text": <full_text str>, "page": <int|None>, "word_count": <int>, "chunks": [<str>, ...], "quit": False, "announcement_wav": <base64 str, optional>, "chunk_wavs": [<base64 str|None>, ...] (optional)}`.

### 2.6 Concrete Scenarios

- **Normal successful scan**: clear page photo ‚Üí two debug JPEGs written, EasyOCR returns multiple detections above 0.30 confidence, page number found via bottom-quarter regex search, full text + word count + chunked TTS returned.
- **GPU init fails** (driver issue, VRAM exhausted, etc.): `_get_ocr()` catches the exception, logs a `model_load` metric with `ok: false`, and retries once with `gpu=False` ‚Äî the very next log line is a successful CPU-mode load; OCR continues to function (slower) rather than the whole feature going down.
- **Nothing detected**: blank/very dark image ‚Üí `raw` is empty list ‚Üí both debug JPEGs still written (they happen before OCR runs), metrics logged with `"error": "no_detections"`, empty text returned, no TTS synthesized.
- **Low-confidence/ambiguous**: all detections exist but fall below `CONF_THRESHOLD = 0.30` ‚Üí `"error": "all_below_threshold"` logged with `detections_total` vs `detections_kept: 0`, empty text returned ‚Äî distinguished from "no_detections" in the metrics for later analysis.
- **Malformed/missing frame**: `frame` key absent or empty string ‚Üí immediate short-circuit, no image decode attempted, no debug files written (this is the one failure path that produces zero disk writes).
- **Bad base64/corrupt JPEG**: decode returns `None` ‚Üí `"decode_failed"` logged, no debug files written (decode failure occurs before the raw-image dump), returns `"Could not decode image."`.
- **Exception during OCR inference**: caught by `try/except Exception` around `_run_ocr`; logged to metrics with the exception's string and returned as `text: f"OCR error: {e}"` ‚Äî never propagates to crash the connection handler.
- **Debug-artifact write failure** (disk full/permission denied): `cv2.imwrite` failure is not explicitly checked (OpenCV returns a bool but the code doesn't inspect it) ‚Äî a failed write would silently produce no file with no exception raised, and processing would continue to OCR regardless (loosely coupled: OCR does not depend on the debug write succeeding). Metrics logging (`_log_metric`) explicitly catches `OSError` so a disk-full condition there is silently dropped rather than crashing.
- **Repeated rapid requests**: `_ocr_lock` serializes construction of the EasyOCR engine singleton (subsequent `_run_ocr` calls reuse the already-built `Reader` without re-acquiring that lock; inference itself is not parallelized across requests, so a burst of scans effectively serializes through the shared model object); the debug directory is self-pruning so rapid scans do not grow disk usage past ~20 files (across raw+proc combined, filtered together by `.jpg` extension, so effectively 10 scans' worth of raw+proc pairs).

### 2.7 Contract & Counterpart

`OCRHandler(FeatureHandler)` sets `name = "ocr"` and implements `handle()`, following the identical pattern to `LidarHandler`. The OrangePi-side counterpart is `ocr_reader.py` ("Book Reader" feature module, documented elsewhere), which captures a camera frame, base64-encodes it as JPEG, and sends `{"feature": "ocr", "type": "scan", "frame": "<b64>"}`; it plays back the `chunk_wavs`/`announcement_wav` audio it receives, or falls back to local TTS using the returned `text`/`chunks` if the server had no synthesis available.

---

## 3. Money Recognition Handler (`handlers/money.py`)

### 3.1 Purpose & Role

Implements a full currency-counting transaction assistant for visually impaired users: it identifies USD and LBP (Lebanese Pound) banknotes in a camera frame via a custom-trained YOLOv8 object detector, tracks a running total across multiple scans within a voice-driven session, computes change/return-bill suggestions, and drives a multi-state voice dialogue (ask currency ‚Üí ask amount ‚Üí confirm ‚Üí scan loop) entirely server-side. This is the most stateful and most domain-specific of the three handlers.

### 3.2 Pipeline Walkthrough

Entry point: `MoneyHandler.handle(msg: dict) -> dict`, `name = "money"`. Unlike the other two handlers, `MoneyHandler.__init__` creates a `Session()` instance per handler instance ‚Äî consistent with `base.py`'s documented contract that one handler instance exists per connection per feature, so session state (`state`, `currency`, `target_usd`, running totals, `undo_stack`, etc.) persists safely across multiple messages in the same connection without cross-talk between concurrent users.

`handle()` reads `msg["type"]`: `"hello"` resets nothing but returns a spoken greeting prompting for currency choice; `"voice"` reads `text` (lowercased) and `frame` (optional base64 JPEG) and delegates to `handle_voice(text, frame_b64, self.session)`; anything else returns `"Unknown message type."`.

`handle_voice()` is a state machine keyed on `session.state`:
- Any message containing a quit trigger word (`QUIT_TRIGGERS`) short-circuits to `{"tts": "Goodbye.", "quit": True}` from any state.
- **`ASK_CURRENCY`**: matches `text` against `CURRENCY_USD`/`CURRENCY_LBP` word sets; sets `session.currency` and transitions to `ASK_AMOUNT`, or re-prompts.
- **`ASK_AMOUNT`**: calls `parse_number(text, currency)` ‚Äî a hand-rolled spoken-number-to-integer parser handling digit tokens, English number words up to millions, comma/dollar/percent-sign stripping, and an LBP-specific heuristic (values under 1000 are assumed to be spoken in thousands, e.g. "fifty" ‚Üí 50,000 LBP). On success transitions to `CONFIRM_AMOUNT`.
- **`CONFIRM_AMOUNT`**: on reject, returns to `ASK_AMOUNT`; on confirm, sets `session.target_usd` (LBP converted via `USD_TO_LBP = 89000`) and transitions to `SCANNING`; otherwise treats the utterance as a new amount.
- **`SCANNING`**: handles `DISCARD_TRIGGERS` (pops `session.undo_stack` via `_undo_last_scan`, reports new running total via `_progress_after_discard`), `REDO_TRIGGERS`/`SCAN_TRIGGERS` (requires a `frame`; if missing or undecodable, returns a spoken error without touching session state; otherwise decodes JPEG via `decode_jpeg` and calls `analyze_frame`), or falls through to a help prompt.

`analyze_frame(frame, session)` is the core CV/ML pipeline:
1. Snapshots current session totals (`_snapshot`) before any mutation, for potential undo.
2. Lazily loads the YOLO model (`_get_model()` ‚Äî singleton behind `_yolo_lock`, device chosen via `torch.cuda.is_available()`, loaded from `MODEL_PATH`, an `ultralytics.YOLO` custom currency-detection checkpoint at a hard-coded path under `Smart_Glove/runs/detect/.../yolov8l_currency-2/weights/best.pt`, overridable at runtime via `configure()`).
3. Runs `model.predict(source=frame, conf=YOLO_CONF_THRESHOLD=0.30, iou=0.5, imgsz=640, augment=False, stream=True, device=...)`.
4. For each result, filters boxes below `BOX_CONF_MIN = 0.40`, builds box dicts with class name, confidence, corner coordinates, x-center, and a coarse `pos_id` (`"<name>_<x1//30>_<y1//30>"`) used to detect whether a bill already seen in a previous scan is still in frame (avoiding double-counting the same physical bill across scans).
5. `filter_overlapping_boxes` removes duplicate/overlapping detections of the same bill (IoU-vs-smaller-box-area ratio > `OVERLAP_RATIO_MAX = 0.70` ‚Üí keeps the higher-confidence box).
6. `r.plot()` (Ultralytics built-in) produces the **annotated** frame with bounding boxes/labels drawn.
7. Class names are parsed as `"<usd|lbp>_<denomination>"` (e.g. `"usd_20"`, `"lbp_50000"`) to compute each bill's USD-equivalent value.
8. New bills (`pos_id` not seen in the previous scan) are distinguished from repeats to avoid re-summing bills still sitting in frame from the last scan.
9. **Debug artifact write**: `dump_frames(raw_frame, annotated_frame, session.scan_number)` always fires whenever `annotated` is non-None (i.e., at least one YOLO result was produced, even with zero boxes) ‚Äî writes the annotated frame to `captured_scans/scan_<n>_<timestamp>_annotated.jpg`, and if `SAVE_BLUR_RADIUS > 0` (default 21), Gaussian-blurs the **raw** (un-annotated) frame via `_blur_frame` and writes it to `captured_scans/scan_<n>_<timestamp>_blurred.jpg` ‚Äî the blurred copy exists specifically to keep a de-identified/privacy-preserving record of the raw camera capture (blurring likely obscures any incidental faces/background/other sensitive content while the annotated copy documents what was detected). It also attempts `cv2.imshow`/`cv2.waitKey(1)` for local debugging, caught in a bare `except Exception` since the server typically runs headless.
10. Logs a metrics record to `money_metrics.jsonl` (`event: "yolo_scan"`, inference time, frame shape, raw/kept/new box counts, mean/min confidence, low-confidence bill count).
11. If no new bills found: updates `last_scan_bills` for future overlap comparisons but does not mutate totals; returns "Same bills detected as before..." or "No bills detected. Please try again." depending on whether any bills (new or repeat) were seen at all.
12. If new bills found: pushes the pre-mutation snapshot onto `undo_stack`, adds new bill values to `session.total_usd`/`total_lbp`, computes remaining balance vs `target_usd` within a dynamic tolerance (`exact_tolerance`: max of ~1 cent-equivalent or 0.5% of target, min-capped at $0.50), and builds spoken feedback: exact match ‚Üí transaction complete + session reset; still short ‚Üí states remaining amount in both currencies; overpaid ‚Üí enters `awaiting_return` change-making mode and calls `suggest_return_bills` (greedy: prefers a single bill matching the change amount within tolerance, else greedily returns bills right-to-left by screen position) plus `speak_return_suggestion` for spoken guidance. If already in `awaiting_return` mode, the new scan is instead interpreted as the customer handing back change, and the logic checks whether the returned value matches the deficit.

### 3.3 Third-Party / Standard Libraries

- `base64` ‚Äî decode incoming JPEG frame (`decode_jpeg`).
- `json` ‚Äî metrics logging (`money_metrics.jsonl`, one JSON object per line).
- `os` ‚Äî path construction (`os.path.dirname(os.path.abspath(__file__))`), directory creation (`os.makedirs(SAVE_DIR, exist_ok=True)`), path joining for all captured-scan filenames.
- `re` ‚Äî strips digit-group punctuation (`re.sub` collapsing decimals) in `parse_number`.
- `threading` ‚Äî `Lock`s guarding lazy YOLO model load (`_yolo_lock`) and metrics file appends (`_metrics_lock`).
- `time` ‚Äî timestamps for filenames and metrics, and inference latency measurement.
- `cv2` (OpenCV) ‚Äî JPEG decode (`imdecode`), Gaussian blur for the privacy-preserving blurred dump (`GaussianBlur`), file writes (`imwrite`), optional live preview (`imshow`/`waitKey`).
- `numpy` ‚Äî buffer wrapping for decode (`frombuffer`), and confidence-statistics aggregation (`np.mean`, `np.min`) for metrics.
- `ultralytics.YOLO` ‚Äî the object-detection model class; `model.predict(...)` runs inference; `r.boxes`/`r.names`/`r.plot()` are Ultralytics `Results` API surfaces used for box extraction and annotated-image rendering. Listed in `requirements_server.txt` as `ultralytics>=8.3.0`.
- `torch` (lazy import) ‚Äî only for `torch.cuda.is_available()` device selection, mirroring the OCR handler's pattern.
- `handlers.base.FeatureHandler` ‚Äî base contract.

Note: `requirements_server.txt` documents `ultralytics` for "YOLO currency detection" consistent with `money.py`'s actual implementation ‚Äî unlike the OCR handler, this file's dependency declaration matches its code.

### 3.4 Key Classes/Functions

- `configure(model_path=None, detect_conf=None, box_conf=None, reliable_conf=None, imgsz=None, tta=None, blur_radius=None) -> None` ‚Äî runtime override of module-level tunables, intended to be called from the server's CLI/startup config (documented elsewhere) before any scan occurs.
- `_get_model() -> YOLO` ‚Äî thread-safe lazy singleton loader.
- `decode_jpeg(b64_str) -> np.ndarray | None` ‚Äî decode helper; logs specific diagnostic messages (`"cv2.imdecode returned None"`, `"JPEG decode error: {e}"`) distinguishing a structurally-bad base64 payload from a valid-but-non-image payload.
- `dump_frames(raw_frame, annotated_frame, scan_number) -> None` ‚Äî **side effect**: writes `captured_scans/scan_<scan_number>_<YYYYMMDD_HHMMSS>_annotated.jpg` always, and `..._blurred.jpg` conditionally on `SAVE_BLUR_RADIUS > 0`.
- `log_metric(rec: dict) -> None` ‚Äî appends to `money_metrics.jsonl`, `OSError`-safe.
- `analyze_frame(frame, session) -> str` ‚Äî the YOLO pipeline; returns a spoken-text string (not a full response dict ‚Äî `handle_voice` wraps it into `resp["tts"]`).
- `handle_voice(text, frame_b64, session) -> dict` ‚Äî the voice state machine; returns `{"tts": str, "quit": bool}`.
- `Session` class ‚Äî per-connection mutable state: `state`, `currency`, `target_usd`, `pending_amount`, `total_usd`, `total_lbp`, `all_bills`, `last_scan_bills`, `awaiting_return`, `return_target`, `scan_number`, `undo_stack`.
- `_snapshot`/`_restore`/`_undo_last_scan` ‚Äî undo mechanism operating on a stack of session-state dict snapshots.
- `exact_tolerance`, `bill_label`, `build_bill_speech`, `filter_overlapping_boxes`, `suggest_return_bills`, `speak_return_suggestion`, `parse_number` ‚Äî pure helper functions for currency arithmetic, NLU, and speech formatting.

### 3.5 Data Structures

Request schema: `type` (`"hello"|"voice"`), `text` (str, required for `"voice"` ‚Äî raw ASR transcript), `frame` (base64 JPEG, optional/required only for scan-triggering utterances).

Response schema: always `{"tts": str, "quit": bool}` ‚Äî this handler never returns structured bill/amount data as separate JSON fields; all detection results (denominations, totals, change owed) are communicated purely as natural-language strings inside `tts`, consistent with the voice-first design of the money-counting feature. (Contrast with OCR, which returns both structured fields and audio.)

### 3.6 Concrete Scenarios

- **Normal successful detection**: clear frame with new, non-overlapping bills ‚Üí annotated+blurred JPEGs written, metrics logged, totals updated, spoken running total or "exact amount" completion message.
- **Nothing detected**: `this_scan_bills` empty ‚Üí "No bills detected. Please try again." ‚Äî no undo snapshot pushed (no mutation occurred), but the annotated/blurred dump still occurs if YOLO produced any result object (even with zero boxes drawn), so disk artifacts exist even for empty detections.
- **Low-confidence/ambiguous**: bills detected above `BOX_CONF_MIN` but below the separate `RELIABLE_CONF` threshold (disabled by default, `0.0`) are still counted toward totals but flagged in speech ("Warning: N bills were not read clearly. Please flatten and rescan.") ‚Äî a distinct concept from OCR's hard confidence cutoff; here, low confidence yields a soft warning rather than exclusion.
- **Same bills re-scanned** (nothing new): tracked via `pos_id` continuity ‚Äî "Same bills detected as before. Add new bills and say scan again," totals untouched.
- **Malformed/missing frame**: `frame_b64 is None` ‚Üí spoken prompt to point camera and retry, no decode attempted; `decode_jpeg` failure (bad base64 or non-image bytes) ‚Üí spoken prompt to check webcam connection ‚Äî neither path touches YOLO or writes any files.
- **Exception during processing**: YOLO inference itself is not wrapped in a try/except in `analyze_frame` ‚Äî an exception there (e.g., model file missing at `MODEL_PATH`, CUDA OOM) would propagate up through `handle_voice` and `handle()` uncaught, meaning (unlike OCR's `_handle_scan`, which explicitly catches inference errors) a money-recognition inference failure would surface as an unhandled exception to the dispatcher rather than a graceful spoken error ‚Äî a notable asymmetry between the two ML-backed handlers worth flagging in the report.
- **Debug-artifact persistence failure**: `cv2.imwrite` return value is unchecked in `dump_frames` (same pattern as OCR) ‚Äî silent failure on disk-full/permission error, no exception raised, transaction logic proceeds unaffected since it doesn't depend on the write succeeding.
- **Repeated rapid requests**: `_yolo_lock` only protects lazy model *loading*, not inference itself ‚Äî concurrent scans from multiple sessions could run `model.predict` simultaneously (the lock is released once `_yolo_model` is set); `scan_number` incrementing and `captured_scans` filenames include both scan number and full timestamp, so collisions are extremely unlikely even under rapid repeated scans within one session.
- **Overpayment / change-making flow**: a distinctive multi-turn scenario unique to this handler ‚Äî after detecting overpayment, session enters `awaiting_return`, computes which physical bills (by position) should be handed back via `suggest_return_bills`/`speak_return_suggestion`, and requires a follow-up scan of the returned bills to close the transaction, looping through `analyze_frame` again with `awaiting_return=True` branching logic.

### 3.7 Contract & Counterpart

`MoneyHandler(FeatureHandler)` sets `name = "money"`, and uniquely among the three overrides `__init__` to construct a `Session()` ‚Äî this is the clearest example in the codebase of the base contract's documented design intent ("one handler instance created per connection per feature, so each handler can hold its own session state"). The OrangePi-side counterpart is `money_recognition.py` (documented elsewhere), which streams ASR transcripts as `{"feature": "money", "type": "voice", "text": "<utterance>", "frame": "<b64 or omitted>"}` messages and speaks back whatever `tts` string the server returns, driving the same conversational state machine from the glove's microphone/camera hardware.

---

## DIAGRAM GENERATION PROMPT ‚Äî Server-Side LiDAR, OCR & Money Recognition Handlers

Create a technical pipeline diagram with three parallel horizontal swim-lanes, one per feature handler, all starting from a shared "TCP dispatcher (feature_server.py)" box at the top that routes an incoming JSON message by its `feature` field. Lane 1, "LidarHandler.handle()" (handlers/lidar.py): show branching on `action` into `_map_save` (base64-decodes `frame`, writes PNG to `lidar_maps/<room_name>.png`), `_pose_update` (converts `x,y` meters to pixels via `_world_to_px`, caches in memory), and `_report` (writes JSON to `lidar_reports/<session_id>_<mode>.json`); show a side box "HTTP viewer :8080 (_start_http)" reading those same files to serve `/live`, `/map/<room_name>.png` (with `_overlay` drawing a red position dot via OpenCV), `/reports`, and `/metrics`. Lane 2, "OCRHandler.handle()" (handlers/ocr.py): show `_handle_scan` decoding base64 `frame` to a JPEG via cv2.imdecode, writing `ocr_debug/scan_<timestamp>_raw.jpg`, running `_preprocess` (auto-rotate, upscale, Gaussian-blur unsharp mask), writing `ocr_debug/scan_<timestamp>_proc.jpg`, then `_run_ocr` calling a lazily-loaded EasyOCR Reader singleton, filtering by `CONF_THRESHOLD=0.30`, running `_detect_page` regex heuristics, then `_chunk_text` and `_synth` producing base64 WAV clips, logging every outcome to `ocr_metrics.jsonl`, and pruning old debug files via `_rotate_debug` (cap 20). Lane 3, "MoneyHandler.handle()" (handlers/money.py): show a `Session` state machine (ASK_CURRENCY ‚Üí ASK_AMOUNT ‚Üí CONFIRM_AMOUNT ‚Üí SCANNING) feeding into `analyze_frame`, which runs a lazily-loaded Ultralytics YOLO model on the decoded frame, filters/deduplicates boxes via `filter_overlapping_boxes`, calls `dump_frames` to write `captured_scans/scan_<n>_<timestamp>_annotated.jpg` (YOLO-plotted boxes) and `captured_scans/scan_<n>_<timestamp>_blurred.jpg` (Gaussian-blurred raw frame for privacy), logs to `money_metrics.jsonl`, and returns spoken running-total or change-making guidance. End all three lanes converging into a shared "response dict {tts, quit, ...} ‚Üí protocol.send_msg ‚Üí OrangePi client" box, and visually distinguish disk-write side effects (file icons with the exact filename patterns above) from in-memory/session-only paths (dashed arrows) to make the persistence behavior of each handler unambiguous.

================================================================================
SECTION 8 ‚Äî HOME AUTOMATION FEATURE (ESP32 RELAYS + QR-CODE WIFI PROVISIONING)
================================================================================

SECTION 8 ‚Äî HOME AUTOMATION FEATURE (ESP32 RELAYS + QR-CODE WIFI PROVISIONING)
===============================================================================

8.1 Purpose and Architecture

Home Automation lets a glove gesture (or voice command) toggle physical
relays ‚Äî lights, an outlet, a fan ‚Äî mounted on a 2-channel ESP32 relay board
somewhere in the house. It is integrated exactly the same way every other
feature is: it is a `Feature` subclass registered in `hub.py`'s `FEATURES`
list, it talks to the server through the existing shared `ServerLink`
(`feature="home"`), and its gestures are stored in `gestures.json` under
`"FEAT:<title>"` keys with zero changes to `gesture_hub/`. Nothing about the
gesture engine, state machine, or enrollment/Programmable-panel flow needed
to change ‚Äî this is a direct payoff of the existing `Feature`/`FeatureRegistry`
design documented in Section 3.

Three independent pieces exist, each owned by a different machine:

  1. **ESP32 relay board firmware** (`server_app/esp_relay_firmware/`) ‚Äî
     Arduino sketch, flashed directly to the ESP32 by the user (not deployed
     through this codebase's Python tooling). Lives next to the server code
     because the server is what generates its setup QR and is what it talks
     to once online ‚Äî it has no relationship to the OrangePi Python client at
     all.
  2. **Server handler** (`server_app/handlers/home.py`) ‚Äî a `FeatureHandler`
     that proxies relay commands from the glove to the ESP32 over the home
     LAN, keeps a small device registry, and hosts a tiny HTTP dashboard
     (port 8090+) that generates the WiFi-setup QR PNG.
  3. **OrangePi feature** (`OrangePI/features/home_automation.py`) ‚Äî two
     `Feature` classes: `HomeAutomation` (a small voice menu: pair / status /
     close) and `RelaySwitch` (one instance per physical relay, each an
     instant, independently gesture-bindable on/off toggle).

8.2 Why Each Relay Is Its Own Feature (not a sub-menu)

`gesture_hub/registry.py`'s `FeatureRegistry.gesture_key(feature)` returns
`f"FEAT:{feature.title}"` ‚Äî a stable key derived purely from the feature's
`title` attribute. Because `RelaySwitch(1, "Relay 1")` and
`RelaySwitch(2, "Relay 2")` are two distinct `Feature` instances with distinct
titles, the existing enrollment walk (`registry.unassigned()`,
`start_enrollment()`, `next_enrollment()`) and the Programmable panel treat
them as two ordinary, independently re-bindable menu items ‚Äî a user assigns
"Relay 1" one gesture and "Relay 2" a completely different one, and can
re-record either at any time from the Programmable panel, with no special
casing anywhere in `gesture_hub/state_machine.py`. `RelaySwitch.run()`
performs its toggle and returns immediately (no blocking loop, no "closing"
gesture needed) ‚Äî functionally a light switch, not a session.

`HomeAutomation` (name="home", title="Home automation") is the third,
separate feature: it owns its own gesture/voice menu for one-time device
**pairing** and for asking the current relay **status** ‚Äî actions that don't
map naturally onto a single instant toggle.

8.3 End-to-End Pairing Flow (QR code path)

  1. Operator runs the server with the home WiFi credentials so it can embed
     them in the QR:
       `python feature_server.py --home-wifi-ssid "MyHomeWifi" --home-wifi-password "secret123"`
  2. Operator opens `http://<server-lan-ip>:8090/qr/relay01` in a browser (or
     the device list at `http://<server-lan-ip>:8090/`) and displays the QR
     on a phone or monitor screen. `handlers/home.py`'s `_generate_qr_png()`
     encodes this exact JSON (the `PROVISION_TYPE` schema, shared verbatim by
     all three components):
       ```json
       {
         "type": "esp_relay_provision",
         "ssid": "MyHomeWifi",
         "password": "secret123",
         "server_host": "192.168.1.50",
         "server_home_port": 8090,
         "device_id": "relay01",
         "relay_count": 2
       }
       ```
  3. On the glove, the user activates Home Automation and says "pair".
     `HomeAutomation._pair()` speaks "Show me the setup QR code now.", opens
     the OrangePi's own camera (the same OpenCV capture pattern used by OCR
     and Money ‚Äî no new camera-handling code, no ESP-side camera needed at
     all), and loops `cv2.QRCodeDetector().detectAndDecode(frame)` for up to
     15 seconds until it decodes a payload whose `"type"` matches
     `esp_relay_provision`.
  4. The freshly-flashed ESP32 is, at this point, broadcasting its own
     open-to-join setup access point (`RelayCtrl-Setup` / `relaysetup`,
     hardcoded identically in the firmware and in `home_automation.py`). The
     OrangePi records its **current** WiFi connection profile name via
     `nmcli -t -f NAME,TYPE connection show --active`, then temporarily joins
     `RelayCtrl-Setup` via `nmcli device wifi connect`.
  5. It `POST`s the decoded JSON straight to `http://192.168.4.1/provision`
     (the ESP32's fixed AP-mode IP) using `urllib.request` ‚Äî no new Python
     dependency needed on the Pi.
  6. Regardless of outcome, it immediately reconnects to the recorded home
     WiFi profile via `nmcli connection up <profile>`. Every step from here
     onward runs over the restored home network.
  7. The ESP32 (`handleProvision()` in the firmware) saves the credentials
     to flash (`Preferences`/NVS) and reboots. On the next boot it finds
     saved credentials, joins the home WiFi as a station, starts its
     relay-control HTTP server, and immediately `POST`s
     `{"device_id","ip","relay_count","fw"}` to the server's
     `/esp/register` endpoint ‚Äî this is how `handlers/home.py` learns the
     board's LAN IP without the OrangePi ever needing to know it. The ESP32
     repeats this registration every 30 seconds as a heartbeat so a later
     DHCP-assigned IP change is picked up automatically.
  8. `HomeAutomation._pair()` speaks a final confirmation once WiFi is
     restored, and logs a `pair` metric (see Section 9) recording total
     elapsed time and success/failure.

Critically, the ESP32 **never** talks to the OrangePi at any point in normal
operation ‚Äî only during this one-time pairing hand-off, and even then it's a
plain unauthenticated HTTP POST to a fixed, well-known setup address. Once
provisioned, every relay command in this system flows exclusively
glove ‚Üí OrangePi ‚Üí feature server ‚Üí ESP32, matching the chosen topology.

8.4 Runtime Relay Toggle Flow

  1. User fires the gesture bound to "Relay 1" (or 2).
  2. `RelaySwitch.run(ctx)` sends
     `ctx.link.send("home", {"action": "toggle", "relay": 1, "state": "toggle"})`
     over the existing `ServerLink` and returns as soon as a reply arrives ‚Äî
     no session, no abort handling needed, matching the "instant switch"
     semantics.
  3. `HomeHandler._toggle()` resolves which physical board to command (the
     most-recently-registered device if `device_id` wasn't given ‚Äî the
     common case with exactly one board), issues
     `GET http://<esp-ip>/relay?ch=1&state=toggle` via `urllib.request` with a
     4-second timeout, and relays the ESP's `{"ok","relay","state"}` reply
     back as a spoken `"Relay 1 turned on."`/`"...off."`.
  4. If the board hasn't registered in over `STALE_DEVICE_S` (120 s), the
     handler still attempts the request (the last known IP might still be
     valid) but appends a spoken hint that the address may be stale if it
     fails, rather than refusing outright.
  5. Every toggle (and status query) is timestamped and logged to
     `handlers/home_metrics.jsonl` ‚Äî see Section 9.

8.5 Concrete Scenarios

  - **Normal toggle**: gesture fires ‚Üí ESP round trip ~10-50 ms on a healthy
    LAN ‚Üí spoken confirmation.
  - **No relay board paired yet**: `_resolve_device()` returns `(None, None)`
    ‚Üí `"No relay board paired yet. Say pair to set one up."` ‚Äî no HTTP
    request attempted.
  - **ESP unreachable** (powered off, moved off WiFi, wrong IP after a DHCP
    change with a missed heartbeat): `_esp_get()` catches
    `URLError`/`TimeoutError`/`OSError` and returns `(None, elapsed_ms)` ‚Üí
    `"Could not reach relay 1."`, optionally suffixed with the stale-address
    hint; the failure is still logged with `ok: false` for the metrics
    report.
  - **QR not found during pairing**: camera never decodes a matching payload
    within 15 s ‚Üí `"No QR code found. Try again with better lighting."`; no
    WiFi switch is attempted at all (the OrangePi's own network connection is
    never touched unless a valid QR was actually read).
  - **`nmcli` missing or the setup AP join fails**: `_nmcli()` catches any
    subprocess error and returns `False` ‚Üí `"Could not connect to the relay
    board's setup network."`; the code never even attempts the WiFi-restore
    step in that specific case since it never left the home network to begin
    with (`joined` is `False`, and reconnecting to the *already-active*
    profile is harmless/idempotent regardless).
  - **Provision POST fails after successfully joining the setup AP** (ESP
    rebooted mid-handshake, malformed JSON somehow, timeout): WiFi is
    restored regardless (the `if profile: _nmcli("connection", "up", profile)`
    call is unconditional once `profile` was captured), and the user hears
